WITTMAN'S GOOD WORK.
CHIEF OF POLICE WITTMAN has begun
his career in his new office by taking ener
getic steps toward the suppression of pool
seliing. In doing so he has naturally aroused the an
tagonism of those who profit by gambling and also of
the yellow journal. He therefore merits and should
have the cordial support of all good citizens.
Concerning the attacks of the yellow journal little
need be said. That paper began by charging the
Chief with a neglect to suppress gambling, and now
that he is engaged in an effort to suppress it the
paper assails him more vigorously than ever. Fortu
nately, as the Chief has nothing to expect from the
favor of that paper, neither has he anything to fear.
Good men understand the situation, and bad men
would be opposed to any officer who sought to put
an end to their vicious practices.
It is well known that a good deal of pool-selling
is carried on secretly under the mask of cigar-selling.
To discover these secret offenders and to prevent
them from violating the law it has been found neces
sary to exercise a police supervision over the cigar
trade generally. The supervision is of course more or
less annoying to legitimate dealers, but they can well
afford to bear the slight annoyance for a time for the
sake of benefits which will accrue from it. For a long
time the legitimate trade has suffered from the com
petition of men who maintain cigar stands and shops
only as a mask for selling pools. As their profits
were derived from pool-selling they were able to
draw away much of the trade that should have gone
to honest and bona-fide dealers. The suppression of
the illegitimate branches of the trade will therefore
tend tp promote the welfare of the honest business.
While the supporters of gambling and pool-selling
are in many respects contemptible, it must not be
overlooked that they constitute a considerable force
in the community. Many a poor dupe who wastes in
pool-buying the earnings of his labor will still side
with the rascals who rob him rather than with the
law that seeks to save him. In order that the per
nicious influence of the sharpers and their dupes may
be counteracted the lesolute strength of rightful pub
lic opinion should be exerted. Chief Wittman should
not be left to make this fight alone. Every man who
respects law and morality, who knows anything of
the evils that flow Irom pool-selling, should stand
with the Chief and earnestly support him in his ef
forts to suppress the sources of the evil.
There is hardly any vice that is more destructive to
the morals of a community than gambling. It cor
rupts every faculty of heart and brain. It leads to
fctud, to drunkenness, to crime and to suicide. . Its
enticements appeal most strongly to the young, and
many of its unfortunate victims nave hardly arrived
at the age of manhood. The profits gained from the
nefarious trade by those who carry it on are enor
mous, and not infrequently they are gained from
money which the victim has stolen from his employer.
Over and over again San Francisco has had to
make a fight in the interests of morality against this
evil. Now we have at the head of the police force
a Chief who has gone about the work of suppression
in the right way. Let him have the public support
which he deserves and he will win, despite all the
pool-sellers may do or their yellow ally may publish.
The Empress Dowager of China lias issued an edict
ordering her subjects to promptly erect a memorial
to Li Hung Chang, and we shall now see whether
they will manage it any sooner than the United States
will manage to construct the national memorial to
McKinley. Of course we are a much swifter people
than the Chinese, but then sometimes an imperial
order starts a movement quicker than an appeal for
subscriptions.
Since women themselves have not been able to
bring about a dress reform in the direction of getting
rid of the long skirts that sweep the pavement, a New
Jersey statesman proposed to help them along by the
enactment of a law prohibiting any woman from
wearing on the street a skirt that does not clear the
ground by at least two inches.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, NOVEMBER 27, 1901.
TROUBLE FOR THE COMBINE.
THE Governor cf Minnesota is first in the field
against the Western railway combine. He an
nounces that if there is at present enough Min
nesota law to keep the Northern Pacific and Great
Northern cut of the combine he will at once invoke
it, and i,f there is not enough he will call the Legisla
ture in special session to make some more. It is an
interesting evidence of the uncertainty as to the law
felt by the engineers of the combination that their
stocks fell off largely as soon as this news came from
St. Paul. If the Governor proceed we may expect a
period of expensive litigation over the legal issue, for
what Minnesota may do other States may, and the
granger agitation of thirty years ago may have an
acute revival.
.The growth of American railways has been on
ward, not on the line of least resistance, but always on
that of the greatest. Beginning in 1828, when the
pioneer road, the Baltimore and Ohio, was started,
the first difficulty encountered was incident to secur
ing right of way. The present method of condemna
tion by use of the right of eminent domain was then
unthought of. The projectors of a railway could not
run their random lines across country, selecting grade
and way and terminal points, and condemning all
right of way for which they could not bargain. If
they came to the line of a stubborn land-owner who
would not sell their project must be abandoned or
changed. Had there been no alternative to the plan
of prurate purchase for right of way it is safe to say
that the system of American roads would never have
been created. One needs to go back to the political
and legal literature of the country from 1828 to 1851 to
learn that the "octopus" is not a new monster in
American politics and law. When it was proposed to
use the right of eminent domain and condemn right
of way the excitement was general and many good
people were convinced that liberty was at an end. In
New Hampshire and other States parties were dis
rupted by it, but everywhere a few enterprising citi
zens firmly stood by progress, and the condemnation
laws were passed by State after State and were firmly
embedded in American law by the decisions of the
courts.
From the beginning of construction in 1828 by 1835
there were in operation 1008 miles. Two years later
New York harbor and Philadelphia were connected
by rail. In 1841 Boston and Albany were put in rail
communication, and the next year Albany and Buf
rslo were connected, and Philadelphia reached the
coal fields by construction of the Reading line. The
panic of 1837 and lack of money for such investments
were seriously felt, and by 1845 there was a slacking
off. In 1848 to 1851 the settlement of the right of way
problem removed one great difficulty, and in 1848
Boston and New York were connected. In 1851 the
Hudson River line was built between New York and
Albany, and that year our total rail mileage was 9021.
In iB6O it was 36,625; in 1870, 52,922; in 1880, 93,262;
in 1890, 166,653: and by 1899 it had reached the grand
total of 191,310 miles, or nearly equal to the combined
rail mileage of all the rest of the world.
As the mileage increased rates of transportation
fell. During the .seventeen years ending 1898 the re
ductions in freight rates saved to the shippers on the
tonnage hauled the vast sum of $3,802,000,000, or one
third the value of all the stocks and bonds of the
roads. The rate reductions in one year, 1897-98,
amounted to a saving to the public of $44,267,874.
In the same period passenger rates fell 21 per
cent. From 1882 to 1898, inclusive, railway mileage
increased 92 per cent and passenger travel only 83
per cent. In that period the reduction in rates to the*
number of passengers hauled amounted to $645,624,
77. So in the seventeen-year period the savings to
the people on passengers and freight by reductions in
rates aggregated $4,447,624,777. During that period
the highest inte r est paid on the railway bonded debt
of the country was 7.77 per cent, and the highest divi
dend on railway stocks was 2.91 per cent.
We have not at hand the mileage of the roads in the
Western combine, nor their gross and net earnings
per mile, but they have been participants in the re
ductions of the seventeen-year period to the extent
of their operation during that time. Now they are to
pass through a period of violent antagonism before
they will be permitted to show that by virtue of the
economy of combination they can increase dividends
without raising rates. The people will never know
the virtue or the vice of the combination until oppor
tunity is furnished for this demonstration.
POLITICAL PROVIDENCE.
THAT worthy guild the Women's Christian
Temperance Union has just been holding its an
nual conventicle at Fort Worth, Texas. Repre
sentatives were present from many countries, v and
the memory of the devoted founder of the order, Miss
Willard, was abundantly honored in the number, in
telligence and zeal of her followers who gathered
there.
On the Sunday that came during the sessions the
thirty-four pulpits of the city were filled by preach
ing ladies, whose sermons were heard with atten
tion by crowded congregations. Members of all sects
were in evidence. The Methodist Episcopal church
South was filled to listen to a Unitarian, and a Meth
odist filled a Presbyterian pulpit. Southern sentiment
was represented by Mrs. Hoge of Virginia and Miss
Belle Kearny of Mississippi, and the voice of England
was raised for righteousness, temperance and the
judgment to come by Miss Christine Tenling of Lon
don.
The star sermon of this great day seems to have
been preached by Miss Greenwood of New York in
the Christian tabernacle. Her subject was, "Preach
ing the Gospel," and she seems to have taken an
armed excursion into politics, a sort of punitive ex
pedition. To this there is no objection. Interest in
public affairs is one of the strong and strengthening
peculiarities of American women. They vote in four
States, and in one of these, Colorado, have taken up
office holding with an energy which makes the tyrant
man stand still with surprise, while they pass him in
the race. They carry into Colorado politics an in
fluence which will command respect, though at pres
ent their status is somewhat misunderstood. In the
last city convention in Denver they were present
as delegates, and manipulated things as skillfully and
as cleanly as they would dough on the kneading
board. In the same convention man was present in
all his variety, and among them was a ward boss,
who indulged in a freedom of off color expression
that was so offensive that one of the ladies said:
"You forget that we are ladies." The captain of
hundreds looked at her in surprise and replied: "
are yees ladies? I tought ye was diligates."
But to return to Fort Worth and Miss Greenwood.
She gave the American male politician a pickling that
he will not soon forget, and in the course of the
operation said: "Politicians strove to make Theodore
Roosevelt Vice President that they might remove
him as Governor of New York, in the hope that they
would no longer be hindered in their wicked work,
and behold, a' most signal and disastrous reversal of
their plans. Ro6sevelt, a man of destiny, becomes
President, while his successor, Governor Odell, de
velops ability to rise above aims and standards of
machine politicians. History emphasizes the exact
ness of God's justice."
Now, we suppose that this ought to be referred
to Senator Platt as a committee of the whole, but
comment may be made in advance of that parlia
mentary disposition of it. It has not heretofore been
thought that Providence takes such an interest in
New York politics as to secure the murder of Presi
dent McKinley in order to rebuke Senator Platt by
the elevation of the Vice President. We arefin doubt
that President Roosevelt takes that view of it. His
min^ perhaps is more finite than that of the inspired
Miss Greenwood, and being so, if impressed with
the wickedness 01 Senator Platt he would doubtless
drop a hint to Providence to get in and convert that
Senator from his wicked ways instead of killing Mc-
Kinley. Why it should be considered a more con
genial job for Providence to kill McKinley than to
soften the heart of Platt and the politicians is what
no man can explain.
Nor is it explained why Providence takes a hand
in New York politics and not in Pennsylvania. Why
should Senator Quay flourish like the bay tree and
Platt wither like the fig tree when attention was
called to its barrenness? Why also should the Rev.
Mr. Swallow, the head of Quay's foes and temperance
leader of bis State, be just turned out of the Meth
odist pulpit for falsehood? Perhaps it shows the
danger of having Providential politics in only one
State and leaving the other forty-four to shift for
themselves.
Miss Greenwood is of the opinion that this "Hiv
tory emphasizes the exactness of God's justice." Bui
still it is left greatly dark to the carnal mind why
McKinley was by that justice condemned to a crutl
death, in order to admonish Platt to the better selec
tion of fourth-class postmasters! If that infinite jus
tice works so indirectly, to the carnal mind again it
appears to be an inferior article to our ordinary brand
of domestic justice. The latter aims at the punishment
of the guilty and the protection of the innocent. It
would let McKinley, the good man, live out his days
in honor and peace, and would indict the wicked poli
ticians by the Grand Jury of the district and send
them to Sing Sing.
By the carnal analysis of this matter, both Presi
dent Roosevelt and Governor Odell need to sleep
with one eye open, foi are they not good men, and
do not the wicked still go up and down the earth
seeking whom they may devour? Is there not danger
that the further admonition of the bad may require
the killing of both President and Governor, in order
to emphasize the divine plan as divulged at Fort
Worth?
THE SUPPRESSION OF NEWS.
AN Associated Press dispatch from Chicago,
published in The Call of yesterday, gave a re
port of statements made in that city by three
members of the Yukon Department of Public Works
concerning the conspiracy to capture Dawson, of
which the first information was given to the world by
The Call. Mr. Maynard, who acted as principal
spokesman for the Yukon party, stated: "We expect
to proceed to Ottawa at once and will have a consul
[ tation with the high Government authorities about
conditions in the territory. This trouble may assume
an international phase, because so many members of
the secret military order are Americans and we can
not try them for treason."
The dispatch stated that the Yukon officials had
stopped at Chicago for the purpose of consulting
with certain capitalists of that city who have large in
terests in Yukon Territory. The White Pass and
Yukon Railway is largely owned by American capital
and its main office is in Chicago. The owners of the
road, along with many other American capitalists,
are of course greatly interested in the conditions of
the country, and the reported conspiracy is a matter
of almost as much concern to them as to the Cana
dians themselves.
That important item of current news, affect
ing large financial as well as political interests, was
sent out by the Associated Press, the greatest news
gathering agency in the world, to all papers entitled
to its service. It was therefore received by our morn
ing contemporaries as well as by The Call, and yet
neithei of them published it. Despite the fact that
the report was furnished by the Associated Press, that
it gave ample authority for its statements, and that it
carried upon its face evidence of its value as a part of
the news of the day, it was deliberately suppressed.
Just what reasons induced our contemporaries to
ignore such statements made by Canadian officials
concerning the conspiracy must be left to conjecture.
It is not likely they will attempt to explain. The fact
that The Call had been first to publish the story may,
however, have had something to do with their desire
to suppress anything which confirmed it. It would
seem that in their judgment it is better to suppress
the news than to admit that another has been first to
oDtain it. /
It will be remembered that a similar practice of
suppression of news was practiced by the Examiner
with respect to wireless telegraphy after the great feat
of The Call in making use of it to report the arrival
of the transport bringing home the California Vol
unteers from Manila, and the success with which The
Call and the New York Herald used it in reporting
the international yacht race. For a time the Exam
iner ridiculed the idea that wireless telegraphy is a
possibility. When experiment after experiment at
last confirmed the new system of telegraphy beyond
any question, then the Examiner ignored the whole
thing. For a long time it would not publish Associ
ated Press dispatches giving reports of the results of
governmental experiments with it. At last, however,
the yellow journal had to admit wireless telegraphy
and publish information concerning progress in its
use.
Something of the same kind will happen in this
case. When The Call first published the great story
of the conspiracy one of our contemporaries ignored
it, while the other ridiculed it. Now they find them
selves compelled to enter upon a policy of suppressing
the news in order to avoid confirming the story.
Perhaps the alternative is not disagreeable to a fake
journal, but to a legitimate newspaper editor the
adoption of a policy which entails the suppression of
important news must be very annoying.
Mayor Tom Johnson of Cleveland, the great re
former who hopes to become President some day, has
made a new reform movement in Cleveland by pro
hibiting turkey raffles, which are so popular during
the holidays, and perhaps if he were President he
would try to prohibit Thanksgiving.
Congress has not yet assembled, but it is already
estimated that the river and harbor bill which will be
presented to it will carry appropriations amounting
to something like $60,000,000, so it would seem the
talk of a reduction of taxes is a little bit premature.

OKLAHOMA TERRITORYâ J. H. L.,
City. The Territory of Oklahoma was
carved out of Indian Territory, to
which was added "No Man's Land," a
strip 170 miles *fcng and thirty-five miles
wide, lying between Texas on the south
and Kansas and Colorado on the north,
so called because not Included in any
State or organized Territory. This strip,
with 6,439,815 acres in Indian Territory
purchased in 1889 for $4,193,799, was thrown
open to settlement by proclamation of thÂ«
President, April 22, 1889. More than 50,000
people entered the first day and the land
was soon occupied. For about a year the
people were without organized govern
ment, but early in 1890 the Territory of
Oklahoma was created. The section of
territory known as the Cherokee strip
was to be added as soon as the title of
the Indians thereto should be extin
guished. In 1891 about 300,000 acres of land
formerly belonging to the Sac and Fox,
Pottawatomie, Shawnee, Cheyenne and
Arapahoe Indians were thrown open to
settlement and in 1893 the Cherokee strip
was also opened. About 90,000 people en
tered this strip on the day named by the
President as the time for the opening of
the lands.

BLANCHE PARTINGTON
THERE should be a regret for every
vacant seat last night at the Grand
Opera-house, and there were many,
for "Die Walkure" was riven in
noble pitch almost from end to end.
The performance was incomparably bet
ter than on Its first production during the
season and aroused an enthusiasm much
more general and genuine than on its first
hearing. The singers were in Inspired
mood, the orchestra to match, and the
Wagner served up last night Lad the
majesty and breadth of Its maker.
Perhaps because little was expected of
Reuss-Belce her Brunnhilde seemed more
nearly to approach the Nordica, concep
tion last night. Certainly the singer was
in better voice than she has been before
since her coming here and sang the part
much more than acceptably. She lacks
Etalellness, power and brilliance, but with
a mood to fit that of the other singers last
night she rose sometimes to a high. level
of accomplishment. Her voice sounded
sweeter and rounder and its quality of
sharp brightness, noted in her Venus,
served her in good stead as the warrior
maid.
Gadskl and Van Dyck shared honors last
night. Van Dyck took Dipple's place as
Siegmund. and particularly in the first act
did great things with the character. He
was at his best vocally and his brilliant
dramatic genius was never more in evi
dence than In that whole wonderful first
act last night. Siegmund's life story, the
growth of his love for Sieglinde, the sword
scene, the simple savagery and primitive
bigness of it all was given in Inspired
fashion, and Mr. Van Dyck's voice held
out to its last colossal necessity. There
was a large drop in quality in his second
act. but there is so little to do compara
tively that it did not much matter.
Gadski was in lovely form, more dra
matic than I have ever seen her and sing
ing at her sweetest. % She also excelled her
self and her voice had a satisfying large
ness and climax that was somewhat
wanting on the first "Walkure" night.
Gadski is an eminent artist and her Sieg
linde is not the least among the splendid
things she has done this season. The lit
tle scene where, in her flight from Hund
ing, she falls fainting into Siegmund"s
arms, was a small masterpiece of acting
arc throughout she has never been better
heard or seen. Schumann-Heink is never
anything but magnificent, and she was
simply immense last night in her all too
small opportunity as Frlcka.
As before Mr. Bispham was the Wotan
âpoor old god!â having the usual hard
time between his royal termagant of a
wife and his willful Walkyrie, Brunnhilde
and repeated his good work of last week'
Mr. Blass was an efficient Hunding and
the Walkyries of the evening, who gave
the "ride" with a meteoric brilliance, were
Schumann-Heink. Van Cauteren, Bauer
meister. Homer, Scheff, Marylli, Seygard
and Bridewell.
To-night one of the star performances
or thÂ« season is announced "Don Pas
quale," with Sembrich. and "I Pagllacci 1

turn tc San Francisco, as she at first
cd. She enjoy* the sunshine daily
and visits all surrounding points of inter
est.
DIAMOND MAN
VISITING CITY
Gardner P. Williams of
Kimberley, South
Africa, Here.
Gardner F. Williams, the pioneer Cali
fornian, who handles more diamonds an
nually and wears less than any other man
la the world, arrived from Kimberley,
South Africa, on Sunday to visit his
mother and sisters, who live in Oakland,
is spending some of his time in San
Francisco. Mr. Williams Is general man
ager of the Kimberley diamond mines and
has under his control an army of 12,000
diamond seekers. He was a resident of
this city from ISSB to 1884, when he went
to Africa.
Mr. Williams was seen at the residence
of T. C. Van Ness, 2845 Fillmore street, by
a Call reporter last night. In the course
of an extended interview he said:
The Kimberley mines are exporting annually
about $25,000,000 worth of. precious stones. Dur
ing the Boer war the mines were shut down for
a long period. The siege of Kimberley was of
lour months' duration, and in that time 8000
shells were fired into the city, with only nine
casualties resulting therefrom. I regard the
war as being practically over. It certainly
would be to the beet lntereEts of the Boers to
submit to the inevitable. The English are
bound eventually to win the struggle, and pro
longed resistance to British authority will re
sult only in making matters worse. The Boers
are incapable of self-government by reason of
their apathy and lack of enterprise
The etatements in the press that Cecil Rhodes
is a dying man are untrue. 1 went shooting
with him in Scotland In September, and he was
able to endure more hardships than m-sclf
He Is a wonderful man. and his railway "tram
sea to sea will certainly be realized. He will
be a factor in the pacification of the Trans
vaal. The conditions of affairs there In conse
quence of the war are deplorable. The entire
country has been devastated and it will require
many years to bring matters to their normal
condition after the peace.
Mr. Williams will remain at the home
of his sister, Mrs. E. B. Clement, 1703
Telegraph avenue, Oakland, until Friday,
when he starts for Africa, via London.

EAERY RODGERS JOINS
RANKS OF TKjZ BENEDICTS
Well-Known Local Advertising Man
Quietly Marries the Widow of
"Ducksey" Rosenbaum.
Mrs. Sarah Rosenbaum, widow of the
late "Ducksey" Rosenbaum, a wealthy
capitalist who committed suicide about a
year ago and over whose estate there
was considerable litigation, was married
yesterday afternoon to Harry A. Rodgers,
a local designer and advertising man. The
couple were introduced about a week ago
by a mutual friend and it was a case of
love at first sight. They left on their
honeymoon last evening and will make an
extended tour of the southern part of the
State.
Mr. Rodgers is well known in local art
and business circles. He is a stylish
dresser and has always been the boon
companion of J. Waldere Kirk, the noted
dude, during the latter's visits on the
coast. The marriage was quite a surprise
to the friends of the couple.
Boys Were Not Robbers.
The case of Frank Band and Louis
Nave, boys, charged with attempting tQ
rob George Hansen at Jackson and San
some streets early Sunday morning, was
dismissed by Judge Cabaniss yesterday.
The testimony showed that the defend
ants and two other boys met Hansen and
his friend and asked them to treat.
Hansen said he had only 15 cents and one
of the boys, not believing him, put his
h>and In his pocket. Just then two officers
appeared and the defendants were ar
rested, the other two boys running away.

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, jnuYEMBER 27, 1901.
NO CONSPIRACY,
SAYS HEYWOOD
Marine Corps Command
ant Testifies at Meade
Court.
NEW YORK, Nov. 26.â P. C. Adsersicn,
a civil engineer, was questioned to-day at
the trial by court-martial of Colonel
Meade of the United States marine corps.
He testified that in his opinion the cost
of the disputed repair work at the navy
yard was excessive. Major Theodore
Wood of the marine corps testified that
while the repair work was crude it ful
filled the specifications.
Brigadier General Charles H. Heywood,
commandant of the marine corps, testified
that he had not spoken to any one of re
tiring before he reached %he age limit,
which will be two years from now, and
that he could not afford to retire at an
earlier day. He denied having an under
standing with any one as to the succes
sion to the office. He stated that he had
not seen or heard anything to indicate a
conspiracy between Colonel Denny and
Major Lauchheimer against Colonel
Meade or any attempt to injure him a,t
headquarters.
"On the contrary," said General Hey
wood, "I think the action taken at head
quarters was such as to make Colenel
Meade a strong candidate for the office of
commandant of the marine corps."
General Heywood said he was perfectly
satisfied with the work done on the ma
rine barracks; the contractors had made
good all defects for which they were
properly responsible. He said that Col
onel Denny had told him that he had on
two occasions been embarrassed while in
specting by the fact that Colonel Meade
was under the influence of liquor, and a
written report on this point was then
made by Colonel Denny at his orders.
TURKISH DIPI/OMAT
MAKES AN EXPLANATION
Embassador to Great Britain Tells of
the Seizure of a
Convent.
LONDON, Nov. 26.â Costaki Anthopulo
Pasha, the Turkish Embassador to Great
Britain, has given out the Porte's expla
nation of the seizure of an Armenian con
vent in the neighborhood of Mush by
Armenian revolutionists and the subse
quent surrounding of the convent by the
Turkish troops. He says that bandits
hay& been ravaging the country and ex
torting money and goods from the Ar
menian population, murdering those who
refused to comply with their demands.
These bandits took about sixty women
and children as prisoners to the monas
tery and Andranik, the leader of the ban
dits, tried to obtain money by threats
from the Armenians of Mush.
Thereupon, in order to protect the wo
men and children, the imperial authorities
surrounded the monastery with Turkish
troops. Some fighting occurred, during
which two brigands and a Turkish cor
poral were killed. British and Russian
Consuls repaired to the scene and testified
to ' the correct attitude of the Turkish
soldiers. The brigands kept up a hot
fire from the monastery and being well
provisioned they were able to offer a stout
resistance.
This official statement from the Turkish
Embassador does not explain how the
affair ended, but a dispatch received sub
sequently from Constantinople says that
the brigands fled and the Turkish troops
were withdrawn.
Wagner's Masterpiece
Fails to Crowd
Theater.
Dispatch Says Calve Will
Not Return to San
Francisco.
WILL PRESENT
A DOUBLE BILL
THIS EVENING
"DON PASQXTAI^E."
BY DONIZETTI.
Norina Mme. Sembrich
Dottore Malatesta . . .Mr. Scotti
Don Pasquale. ..Mr. Tavecchia
Notaro Mr. Vanni
Ernesto Mr. Salignac
"I PAGLIACCI."
BY LEONCAVALLO.
Nedda Miss Fritzi Scheff
Canio Mr. de Marchi
Silvio .Mr. Declery
Beppe Mr. Reiss
Tonio Mr. Campanari
Conductor, Mr. Flon.
THE large audience that was ex
pected to be present last night to
hear the second performance of
"Die Walkure" by the Grau com
pany at the Grand Opera-house
failed to materialize.
Just why society did not turn out in full
force for the superb presentation puzzled
the management and the Wagnerites.
Either grand opera is a social fad, as
Manager Grau himself has said, and the
novelty of gala nights has worn away, or
else the indisposition of Eames, Sander
son and Calve has created alarm and the
public fears to put up its dollars as it did
during the first two weeks of the season.
The Grand Opera-house was a little
more than half filled last night. There
was no elaborate display of dress and
promenading in the lobby and foyer was
confined to the men In the audience.
The artistic work of Gadski, Schumann-
Heink, Bispham, Reuss-Belce and Van
Dyck was rewarded with considerable ap
plause and the great "Valkyrie chorus"
elicited the heartiest approbation. The
work of Damrosch and the orchestra was
well worth a visit to the theater and the
conductor was called to the footlights
with the singers.
The management announced last night to
the press that it hoped to present Eamea,
Sanderson and Calve as per programme
and that seats for the rest of thi3 and next
week would be placed on sale at the box
office of the opera-house to-day.
Grau has arranged to present Calve
on Saturday night in "Carmen."
A representative of Manager Grau
stated last night that no word had been
received from Calva, but that there was
every hope that she would be back in
San Francisco to sing- on Saturday night.
A big house is expected to-night when
a double bill will be given, consisting
of "Don Pasquale" and "I Pagliacci."
Sembrich, Scotti, Tavecchia, Vanni and
Salignac are to sing in "Don Pasquale"
and Fritzi Scheff, De March!, Declery,
Relss and Campanari are to be heard In
the delightful composition of Leoncavallo.
The management announces- "Romeo et
Juliette," with Sybil Sanderson, for the
matinee to-morrow, to be given at popular
prices, while the bill for to-morrow even
ing is "Die Meistersinger," with its great
cast.
Eames la expected to sing on Friday
night in "Alda' and the Saturday matinee
is to be "II Barbiere di Siviglia," with
Sembrich in the cas^
BLAINE MARSHAL
FIRES INTO MOB
One Man Is Dying and
Another Seriously
Wounded.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BLAINE, Wash., Nov. 26.â Strict atten
tion to duty nearly cost Night Marshal
Doane of Blame his life last night. While
arresting an Italian laborer he was set
upon by a number of the offender's ccmn
trymen, armed with knives and axes.
After warning them to desist Doane fi' ed,
emptying six chambers of his rev ever
into the mob. Two Italians were hit and
one of them will die. He was shot three
times, through the neck, lung and shoul
der. Another Italian was shot through
the leg. They were carried to the city
jail and to-day sent to the hospital fn
Whatcom. Doane was not badly injured.
For some time an attempt has been
made by the authorities to compel Italian
fishermen and loggers in this vicinity to
refrain from carrying concealed weapons.
Some Italians have obeyed, but others
have refused. Orders were accordingly is
sued to search all Italians when they
came to town and take their weapons
from them. The object was to prevent
their fighting among themselves. Doane's
life had been threatened because he made
strenuous efforts to disarm all Italians
found carrying weapons.
WOMAN DIRECTOR
IS NOT POPULAR
Teachers Are Opposed
to Mrs. Kincaid's
Reappointment.
Principal O'Connor Denies
Circulating Petition in
Her Behalf '
A choice morsel of gossip was tossed
about among the city's school teachers
yesterday. It was to the effect that a pe
tition would be circulated among the
pedagogues for signature, asking Mayor
elect Schmltz to reappoint Mrs. Mary
Kincaid as a member of the Board of
Education.
It was stated that Principal Joseph
O'Connor of the Mission High School
would take the petition around and "re
quest" the teachers to append their names
to it
A detail of the story was that O'Connor
would be backed up. in the affair by a yel
low morning journal that had sent him
around the world. Gossip reported that
the yellow journal, having aided Mayor
elect Schmitz in his campaign, would de
mand the reappointment of Mrs. Kincaid.
Teachers who were seen yesterday were
averse to talking for publication, but
Principal O'Connor was willing to be
quoted. He said:
" This story is made out of whole
cloth. There is not a word of truth in it.
I have had absolutely nothing to do with
the circulation of any petition asking for
the reappointment of Mrs. Kincaid. I
defy any one to come forward and say
that I am connected with any such propo
sition. It is against the rules of the de
partment and I do not know of any such
petition being circulated. I am glad of
this opportunity of making a statement
and wish to go on record in the mat
ter.'
The school teachers are awaiting
anxiously the time when Mayor-elect
Schmitz will assume office. The women
teachers are said to be almost unanimous
ly opposed to the reappointment of Mrs.
Kincaid.
SATISFIED MRS. STEPHENS.
ENDED HER OWN EXISTENCE
Captain Seymour, After Making a
Thorough Investigation, Props
the Case.
After making a thorough investigation
into the allegation made by Frank Spen
cer, brother of the late Mrs. Charles A.
Stephens, that she did not commit sui
cide, Captain Seymour is satisfied that
Spencer is wrong. Yesterday he h^ard
his statement, which was altogether based
on talk and suspicion, and also the state
ment of the dead woman's husband, who
characterizes the suspicions cast upon
his parents as a gross outrage. His
mother had not been in the city since a
week before last Sunday and his father
is a decrepit man, 70 years of age.
The stomach of the dead woman was
sent to the cicy chemist for anlyals after
the autopsy had shown acid poisoning,
probably hydrochloric acid. Meantime
Captain Seymour is satisfied that the ver
dict of the Coroner's jury Monday was
correct and will do nothing further in the
matter.
Police Officer Fined.
Ex-Police Officer Frank Green, who was
dropped from the force on June 25 last for
failing to report at a fire, applied to th*
Police Commissioners last night for rein
statement.
Officer 4 R. H. Beamer pleaded guilty to
falling asleep on duty In the White Star
laundry and was fined $10. The cases of
Officers Barry and RoDcrt Lean were con
tinued for a week.
For the fifth time the Commissioners
refused a license to open a grill and sa
loon at the corner of Beale and Market
streets. There were a number of peti
tions and protests filed by business men
in the neighborhood, the protests out
weighing the petitions.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
New Yo'-k Exchange unsettled and nominal Sterling weak.
Silver continues to decline. Mexican dollars lower.
Wheat quieter and easier. Freights firm.
Barley, Oats. Corn and Rye unchanged. .
Bran and Middlings lower. Hay unchanged.
Beans quieter, with less fluctuation.
Many contract Potatoes coming in. Onions scarce and higher.
Butter tophcazy. Cheese steady. Eggs begin to weaken. .
Cranberries cleaning up well.
Evaporated Apples higher. Other Dried Fruits unchanged.
Xuts. Raisins and Honey as before.
An advance in Lard goes into effect to-day.
Hams and Bacon show no change.
Tallow higher and very scarce. Hides continue firm.
li'ool market in very fair shape. Hops still dull.
Beef, Mutton and Pork as previously quoted.
Dressed Turkeys in fair receipt and steady.
Game selling at steady prices.
Good Thanksgiving demand for Fresh Fruit.
Local stocks and bonds fairly active.

The Weather and Crops.
The weekly report of A. G. McAdie, section
director of the climate and crop service of
the weather bureau, ts as follows:
GENERAL SUMMARY.
The temperature has been slightly above
normal in all parts of the State during the
week. Heavy legs have prevailed along the
coast and in portions of the San Joaquin Val
ley, and rain has fallen from Fresno north
ward, the greatest precipitation having oc
curred in the counties about the bay. No
injurious frosts have been reported. .
Early town wheat is in excellent condition
and growing rapidly in all sections. Summer
lallowing is nearly completed in some localities
and larwers are preparing for. winter grain
planting. If the season continues lavorable
me acreage sown to grain will be largely in
creasea in many places. The heavy rain re
tarded plowing in some of the northern bay.
counties, but in most places this work is go
ing lorward rapidly. Grass. is making rapid
growth and sreen leed is more plentlrul man
lur several years at this date. Stock is in
excellent condition. Beans are nearly all
feathered and the crop has turned out remark
ably well, the loss from rain having been
much less than anticipated.
Orchards and vineyards continue in good con
dition ana tree pruning is in progress. The
orange crop is being gathered and shipped.
Oranges are 01 better quality than usual, and
11 some sections the yield will exceed that of
last season. Olive picking and pickling are
progressing.
&AÂ»_Â«.AaiÂ£NTO VALLEY.â weather dur
ing the week has been lavorable for farm work,
The temperature has been somewhat above the
normal and light rain has fallen in nearly all
sections. Plowing and seeding continue. Wheat
is making splendid growth and looks strong
ana heaitny. Summer-tallowing is nearly com
pleted in some places and farmers are prepar
ing for winter sowing. Grass is growing rap
idly and green feed is unusually abundant.
Stock is in good condition. Orchard work is
progressing. Oranges and olives are being
gathered. The bean crop has been harvested.
COAST AND BAY SECTIONS.â Warm
weather has prevailed during the week, favor- '
able lor l&rm work and crowing crops. Rain
has fallen from San Francisco northward,
amounting to over two inches in Napa and
Sonoma counties, and heavy fogs have pre
vailed along the coast. Rain is needed In the.
southern coast counties, where the soil in some
places is too dry for plowing. Some cultivat
ing is being dene in tnese places and in other
sections plowing and seeding are progressing
rapidly. It the season continues favorable a
large acreage of wheat will be sown. Green
Seed is in excellent condition. Some orchardlsts*
in Napa County are wrapping prune trees with
paper saturated with "bug exterminator," hop
ing to prevent the ravages of the cankerworm.
SAN JAOQUIN VALLEY.â fogs have
prevailed in most sections during the nights
&nd mornings, clearing away about 10 o'clock.
Light rain has fallen from Fresno northward,
in some places having been sufficient to benefit
early grain and pasturage. Light frosts have
occurred in some sections. Early sown grain
is growing rapidly and looks well. The wheat
acreage will be materially increased in portions
of Tulare County. Plowing and seeding are in
progress except or. the very dry lands. Green
feed is plentiful and stock is in good condition.
Orchards and vineyards are looking well.
Orange picking and shipping continue.
SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA.â The tempera
ture has been nearly normal during the week
and no rain has fallen. Fogs have prevailed
along the coast. The bean crop is practically
all harvested; there have been some losses
through rain and fogs, but not so great .as
anticipated. Plowing and seeding are progress
ing in nearly all sections: a large acreage is
being sown to barley in the vicinity of Ana
heim, and there will be about the usual acre
age, of v.heat if conditions continue favorable.
Early sown grain and pasturage are in good
condition, but will need rain : soon. Oranges
art ripening rapidly; there will be a good crop.
A . few late raisins are on â¢ the trays in San
Diego County.
LOS ANGELES SUMMARYâ fine
growing week. Plowing and seeding progress
ing actively. Beans are nearly all thrashed
and are turning out better than was expected.
Hills and valleys are covered with green feed.
EUREKA SUMMARY.â PIowing and seeding
continue Some grain is up and making good
progress. Pasturage is excellent. Farmers are
chipping apples and potatoes.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The disturbance on the Washlnffton coast
continues and conditions are favorable for a
southerly extension. During the past twelve
hours the pressure has risen along the coast
between Cape Mendocino and Cape Blanco. It
will probably fall Wednesday.
Except in the extreme northwest portion of
California no rain has fallen.
Heavy rain has fallen at Neah Bay.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. November 27, 1901:
Northern California â Cloudy and unsettled
weather Wednesday, with showers along the
coast north of San Francisco; fresh southerly
winds.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday, becom
ing cloudy at night; fresh easterly winds.
Nevada â Cloudy Wednesday; fresh easterly
winds.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Cloudy and un
settled weather, probably showers; fresh south
east winds. increasinE in force.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE.

EASTERN MARKETS.
New York Stock Market.
NEW TORK, Nov. 26.â Amalgamated Copper
went through an exceedingly varied course of i
movements in to-day's stock market and fin- I
ished unchanged from last night. The course
of this stock was a pretty accurate index of
the general market to-day. The day's net
changes are seen to be very small, as a rule,
and considerably mixed, although the extreme
range of prices was considerable. A notable
fact in connection with the constantly changing
course of prices was the consistency of the
movements. Sometimes the course of the mar
ket would vary distinctly three times within
an hour, traversing each time an average range
of nearly a point. Yet the whole list moved
pretty much in unison, there being no appar
ent conflict in the tendency of the prominent
active stocks. The opening dealings reflected
a distinct effort at a bull demonstration for
an advance, the trunk lines being selected for
leadership. Amalgamated Copper was also sup
ported in the opening dealings, but the weak- |
ness which quickly developed in this stock was j
reflected In the whole market, and so with its
subsequent changes. The stock tell by succes
sive str.-.-es and very uncertainly Vp 78 and re- !
coverec vith frequent reactions to 80%, ending
at SO. There was an entire lack of news to !
account for the movements. The early weak- .
ness was in face of permission from a court to
the Boston and Montana Company to pay the i
dividend on that stock under bond. The with- j
holding of these dividends was officially in- ;
Terred as a cause for the reduction of the divi
dend on Amalgamated Copper. The recovery ;
In the stock was- in spite of the sharp break In
the price of raw copper in London, following
some recent speculative buying In that mar
ket. A striking feature of the check to the
recent advancing tendency of the market was j
the decided decrease in activity. To-day's
transactions were abcut half a million shares
less than those of yesterday. The unsettling
influence of the apparent demoralization in
Amalgamated Copper was supplemented to a
degree by a sharp recovery in foreign exchange, ;
which awakened some renewed questioning over j
the resources of the money market for the re- ,
mainder of the year. The lessened supply of I
exchange is doubtless due to the completion of
realizing on what might be called speculative
purchases of exchange which have been recent- |
ly made. An advance of seven points in Gen- [
eral Electric, the strength of Sugar and Man- j
hattan and the heaviness of Baltimore and
Ohio and St. Louis and San Francisco were '
notable.
Railroad bonds were Irregular; total sales,
$3,725,000. United states bonds were all un
changed on the last call.

London Market.
NEW YORK, Nov. 26.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The stock market to-day was inactive and
depressed by the forced selling of mines and by
a fresh break in English rails.
The American department was dumb until
the afternoon and nominally under parity, save
in the case of Ontario and Western. The open
account is trifling. . NÂ«jÂ»r York came weak, then
caused a rally by ilbeftl purchases of Wabash.
The close was steady.
The market is waiting for ' President Roose
velt's message to Congress, particularly his
treatment of the tariff question. A leading ar
bitrageur considers the outlook dull, until the
Northern Securities Company stock is ready for
the market. Rio Tlntos touched 45 on the drop
in Amalgamated Copper. Silver sold at 25% on
New York realization.

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO. Nov. 26.â The corn market, which
absorbed most of the Interest, opened In the
face of lower cables with a steadiness which
surprised even the bulls. December, having
started %@%c lower at 62%@62c, was offered
very sparingly and on buying to cover scattered
short lines the price early worked up to 62% c.
The out-of-town buying movement, which has
done much to sustain prices recently, was of
a less urgent character on the advance, how
ever, and during the afternoon December fell
to 61% c and closed easy, %@%c under yester
day, at 61%Â«61%c. May was better supported
and while the price was reduced its premium
over the near delivery was Increased by %c.
The wheat trade was bare of features, prices
following every movement of the corn market.
December' opened %@%c lower at 72%@72c ori
easy cables and large Northwest receipts. Un
|er the steadying influence of the early brace
m corn the market worked up to 72% c and
later, still following the coarse cereal, It de
clined to 71%'372c, at. which price the market
closed. 94c under yesterday.
Bradstreet's world's visible showed an In
crease of 7.000,000 bushels.
Oats were easier, with the trade more broken

New York Metal Market.
NEW YORK, Nov. 26.â The supply of tin
continues scarce, and as traders are anxious
to purchase, prices again advanced with the
feeling strong at the close. The net gain was
60 points with buyers at $29 85 for spot. At
London values took another upward course,
gaining Â£1 2s 6d for spot, and futures clos
ing strong at Â£117 2s 6d and Â£109 respec-
Trading in both markets was fairly active.

dation, closing easy with spot quoted at Â£65
16 3 3d and futures at Â£63 16s 3d. Here the
market was quiet and unchanged at $16 85@17
for lake and $16 37%@16 62% for casting.

and Â£11 15s, and the latter at $4 30 nominal
and Â£16 15s respectively.

London Wool Sales.
LONDON. Nov. 26.â The sixth series of the
1901 wool auction sales opened to-day with a
very large attendance. Competition was slow,
as the offerings, which numbered 10,755 bales,
were rather inferior. The offerings consisted
principally of cross-breds and faulty Merinos.
A few fine qualities were offered and caused
active bidding, finally selling at unchanged
rates. All lower grades were 5@7% per cent
lower.

LOCAL MARKETS.
Exchange and Bullion.
The banks auoted no rates for New York
Exchange yesterday, as the market was very
unsettled. Now that tax time has passed,
however, it is expected that rates will soon
get back to normal conditions. Silver continues
to ease off, Sterling Exchange is still weaker,
and Mexican dollars are quoted much lower.
The steamer Sierra from Sydney brought up
$486,860 in sovereigns, making a total from this
source thus far this year of $11,941,413, against
$17,104,364 for the same time last year.

WHEAT FREIGHTSâ The market is active,

6 to 35s 9d, according to size of vessel, usual
options, greatly reducing the disengaged ton
nage In port. The chartered wheat fleet in port
now has a registered tonnage of 69,360, ftfainat
51,615 tons on the same date last year; disen
gaged, 6512 tons, against 12,653; on the way to
this port, 193,750, against 122,376.
WHEATâ The foreign markets were quieter

plies: East of the Rockies, an Increase of
6,541,000 bushels; Europe and afloat, an Increase
of 1,400,000; total increase. 6,941,000 bushels.
Chicago was weaker in sympathy with the re
actionary tendency in coarse grains,, chiefly
due to speculative realizing, and the larger in
terior movement in Oats. The bears were also
encouraged by the heavy movement in the
Northwest. Bradstreet's statement, published
above, coupled with the large increase in the
American visible supply during the past two
weeks, cave the bears expectations of a de
cline. Some long wheat came out on large re
ceipts In the Northwest, and trading was not
as heavy a? during the past few days.
Against these bearish conditions, however, St.
Louis was higher and Kansas City wired in
jury from dry weather in some parts of Kan
sas and Missouri. The other markets were rel
atively strong, and there were plenty of buying
orders. Minneapolis mills reported sales of
45,000 barrels Flour.^ Outside orders absorbed
all the wheat sold during the morning.

KLONDIKE PLOT
United States Does Not
Sanction the Bold
Conspiracy.
If Necessary Troops Will Be
Stationed on Border to
Prevent Filibustering.
Special I>ispatch to The Cai!.
CALL BUREAU. 1406 G STREET. N
W., WASHINGTON, Nov. Itâ No infor
mation has been received by the State
Department regarding the alleged pur-
A. Â¦ American miners and others to
Usfa a republic in the Northwest Ter
anada. and no i ll nUlul ccn
t.Â£ this movement has been made to
State Pepartment by Lord Paunce-
Brttfah Embassador.
The movement will not receive ihe of-
Bupport of the United States. Fur
thermore. action will be taken to prevent
liiibustering expedition? from organizing
on Alaska.: soil and marching into Canada.
If necessary orders will be given to the
troops in Alaska t< ions on th^
dary line and prevent any violation of
the neutrality laws. The authorities do
not believe the movement is important
and they expect that as soon as the win
ter is over th* Canadian police will dis
perse the men involved in the affair.
OTTAWA. Nov. 26. - Superintendent
Primrose of the mounted police, who has
been in charge of the Dawson district for
some time past, arrived in the city this
morning on three months' leave of ab
sence. Superintendent Primrose was one
of those who advised the Government of
the report that reached him of a scheme
to capture Canadian territory in the Yu
kon. He afterward said that he was un
able to confirm it. Superintendent Prim
rose reported to Comptroller White of the
mounted police here and the latter after
ward stated that the last had been heard
of the Yukon scare.

DRILLING SHIP IRIS
SUFFERS NO DAMAGE
United States Vessel That Went
Ashore Near Iloilo Is Again
Afloat.
MANILA. Nov. 26.-The United States
drilling ship Iris, which, as cabled No
vember 24. had then been aground on a
reef near Iloilo, island of Panay. -for three
days, which was succored by the cruiser
New York and by the gunboat Yorktown,
has been floated. It is believed that she
did not sustain any damage.
Patterson, an Englishman, the secre
tary of Sixto Lopez, who was smuggled
ashore by Fiske Warren of Boston, was
taken before the Collector of the Port,
with Morgan Shusler, when he called at
the Custom-house for his baggage.' The*
Collector requested that he take the oath
of allegiance, and as Patterson refused to
do so he will be deported.
Battle Expected With Convicts.
LEAVENWORTH. Kans., Nov. 26-
Warden McClaughery has received word
of the capture at Tuscumbia, Ala., of
James Seymour, one of the convicts who
escaped during the mutiny. Captain Tel
ford leaves to-night to bring him back.
Word has also been received that a posse
of Deputy United States Marshals have
surrounded Keating and Murray in the
Shawnee hills, Indian Territory. These
men are desperate convicts and a battle
is expected.
POSTXTM CEREAL.
COFFEE IMPORTER
Tells Some Plain Facts.
One of the heaviest importers of coffee
in America and who requests that his
urn* be kept from the public in connec-
tion with the following subject because of
the effect it would have on his business,
Bays : 'I have used coffee for over thirty-five
years, but about a year ago was compelled
to discontinue its use on account of its
effect on my health. Since that time I
have used nothing in its place but Postum
Cereal Food Coffee, and properly pre-
pared it Is simply delicious with cream
and sugar.
"In connection with this I have also
used Grape-Nuts Breakfast Food. - While
in Florida this winter I carried a pack-
age with me all the time, so if I was un-
able to get what I wanted for breakfast
I could rely on my own supply.
"Any one who could "have known of my
condition a year ago, and the very great
improvement now, would have no cause
to question the wisdom of my change
from the old-fashioned diet to the pres-
ent."

BRITAIN'S WAR
PLEASES EUROPE
Powers Have No Desire
for Attempt at In
tervention.
United States Not Likely to
Intercede for the
Eoers.
f pccial Dispatch to The Call.
CALL BUREAU. 1406 G STREET, N.
W., WASHINGTON. Nov. 26.-Despite the
! apparent desire of the powers of con
i tinental Europe that the United States
I should intervene- to stop the war in South
! Africa, the administration officials ex
! press the opinion that those governments
! really prefer its continuance.
This belief is based upon the fact that
i the prolongation of the struggle weakens
Great Britain and makes the European
nations relatively stronger. It is true that
I unofficial suggestions have been advanced
,to the United States to interfere, but
these suggestions have always been met
, with the response that the scene of the
war is $000 miles away from this country
'Â¦ and there is no excuse for intervention.
Great Britain has been extremely
friendly to the United States. She has
without hesitation granted important con
cessions, notably in the matter of the
: isthmian canal treaty, and she has
promptly settled all questions which have
arisen in connection with the South
African war.
The latest case to which her attention
has been called is that of Ivenon Porter,
an American boy. who is serving in tho
British army. Young Porter's father
claimed that he had been employed as a
muleteer on a vessel transporting British
mules to South Africa ana that he was
practically forced to enlist in the British
army. An investigation made by the
State Department established that no
pressure was applied to young Porter and
that he enlisted of his own free will. It
is emphatically denied that Great Britain
has a recruiting station in this coun
try. The British Government is now in
quiring into the case of Porter. He will
probably be discharged from the British
army and returned to his parents.
HOLDS IT A VIOLATION
OF LAWS OF MONTANA
Governor Toole Makes a Statement
About the Northern Securities
Company Plan. _
HELENA. Mont.. Nov. 26.â Governor
Toole to-day replied to the request of
Governor Van Sant of Minnesota for his
views on the absorption of the stock of
the Northern Pacific, Great Northern and
Burlington railways by the Northern Se
curities Company. Governor Toole says
that according to the interpretation of
Governor Van Sant of the objects of the
Securities Company the absorption is a
plain violation of the constitution and
laws of Montana. He says he does not
now know what legal means can be taken
to defeat the objects of the consolidation,
but whatever they are they will be I
brought into operation. *
The Governor says the constitution of
Montana plainly forbids just what the at
torneys of the Northern Securities Com
pany have accomplished. He intimates
that the company is trying to do mdi- j
rectly what it could not under the Mon- i
tana law do directly, and he says that j
while the plan appears to be a successful
evasion of the law, it shall not be so taken
until the court of last resort has passed
upon the question. He promises the ac
tive aid of the Attorney General of the
State and says that later he will fix a
date for meeting and conferring with Gov
ernor Van Sant.

PARIS. Nov. 26.â The Chamber of Dep

Martin Hogan.
CHICAGO, Nov. 26.â Martin Hogan, the
Irish patriot, died to-day at the County
Hospital. He was 93 years of age. Ho
gan, together with others interested in
the Fenian movement, was convicted of
treason In 1866 and transported to the
English penal colony in Australia. In
1869 Hogan and his companions, except
ing John Boyle O'Rellley, who had pre
viously escaped, were rescued by a boat
which had been fitted out for the* purpose
by Irish sympathizers.

Surveyor General James M. G'eaves, Who
Took Deep Interest in California's Rivers
and Harbors, Dies After an Operation
JAMES MALCOMB GLEAVES,
United States Surveyor General of
the State of California, died at
Waldeck Sanatorium at f o'clock
this morning.
James Malcomb Gleaves had Â» been a
resident of this city for four years and of
the State for twenty-seven years. His
residence here was at 899 Pine street. He
was born in Guernsey County, Ohio, on
September 10, 1852, and was educated in
the University of Missouri, to which State
he went when a boy. He taught school
for a time after graduating and in 1574
came to California and taught here for a
short period. In 1875 he settled in Shasta
County, following the occupation of a
mining engineer. Later he was elected
Surveyor of that county. In 1894 he was
elected State Senator from the Second
District, on the Republican ticket, over
SETTER GUARDS
RUNAWAY CHILD
Victoria Martin, Aged
1, Starts Out to See
the World.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
118 Broadway, Nov. 28.
A pretty little girl and an Irish setter
were the first passengers this morning in
the police patrol wagon. They had both
str&yed from their homes and when weary
of wandering had shared a bed under the
stars in a vacant lot at the corner of
Forty-seventh street and San Pablo ave
nue.
Victoria Martin is 11 years old. Her
father is dead. Her mother "works in
the city." Victoria is "boarded out," as
she calls it, with Mrs. M. E. Strange, a
colored woman, living at 1771 Goss street.
Instead of going to school Victoria de
voted yesterday afternoon to seeing the
world. She Invaded territory to her un
known and by evening the little girl was
completely lost. She had formed the ac
quaintance of the setter during the after
noon and night found them still together.
Victoria had treated the dog's advances
with patronizing condescension, but as
darkness fell the relations changed and
by the time the stars were all hung out
doggie was in full charge of the expedi-
When Mrs. Dr. H. E. Rowell looked
out this morning on the vacant lot ad-
Joining her residence she discovered Vic
toria and her guardian. They were lying
side by side. The little girl was .still
asleep, cuddled close to her warm coated
friend, with her arms clasped tightly
around his neck.
The dog resented Mrs. Rowell's inter
ference, but when Victoria awoke they
both enjoyed the breakfast that was soon
provided for them.
Mrs. Rowell notified the police and dog
and girl were transported to the City
Prison, where Victoria was later claimed
by Mrs. Strange and taken home. The
dog, finding his charge in good hands, re
sumed his wanderings alone.
HUSBAND AND WIFE
HURT IN RUNAWAY
J. Sorenson Dragged a Block and His
Spouse Pitched Out of Their
Buggy.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â J. Sorenson, fore
man for Wellman. Peck & Co., San
Francisco, wholesale grocers, and his
wife were badly bruised this evening In
a runaway accident' on Washington
street. Sorenson was driving a spirited
horse along the thoroughfare when he
noticed that one of the traces had
slipped from the singletree of the buggy
shafts. He quietly turned into the curb
just north of Thirteenth street to re
fasten the tug. Just as he stepped out
the horse jumped and tore up Washing
ton street, dragging Sorenson along the
street, his wife In the buggy screaming
jw^th fright. At Fourteenth street Ihe
horse ran Into a telegraph pole, throw
ing Sorenson under the buggy wheels and
pitching Mrs. Sorenson headlong over the
dashboard. She landed on the horse's
back, and that saved her life.
Assistance was quickly at hand. After
recovering sufficiently Mr. and Mrs.
Sorenson went to their residence at 1009
Pine street. The buggy and horse were
uninjured. _^

CHIEF ROOMS
CHARGES FRAUD
Accuses Pound Officials
of Looting the City-
Treasury.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â According to the
report made by Chief of Police Hodgkins
to-day to City Clerk Church, Poundmas
ter Swift and his deputies have been for
some time past successfully operating a
scheme for looting the city treasury by
means of false returns.
The law allows the poundmaster $1 50 a
head for all dead pigs picked up within
the city limits. The average harvest
prior to Poundmaster Swift's incumbency
was about fifty pigs per annum. In 1900,
however, the pound received $1 50 a head
for 349 dead pigs removed from the streets
and this year up to November 12 the total
is 878.
Police investigations, which are borne
out by the confession of Deputy Pound
master Frank McClelland, show that the
pound officials have been collecting infant
pigs in all directions, drawing largely on
the refuse of various slaughter houses.
The crop was at times too large for the
market and the police report tells how
the surplus was stored in pickle and
drawn on from time to time to keep up
with the financial requirements of the
pound management.
The City Council will take action on the
report at its next meeting.

TWO MERCHANTS
FACE COURT'S IRE
Contempt Proceedings
Sequel to Peculiar
Robbery.
Cayucos Business Men Are
Fighting for Their Liberty
and Honor.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Nov. 26.â From the lit
tle town of Cayucos. San Luis Obispo
County, comes a sensational legal case in
volving the â¢ honor and liberty of two
prominent business men. It is being heard
in the United States District Court of this
city, where Judge Olin Wellborn is trying
three persons for contempt. The story in
volves the sudden T^iilure of a supposedly
rich firm, the disappearance of many
thousands of dollars, the peculiar robbery
of a Â«afe which was not injured and a
mysterious woman who has exerted an
influence over one of the partners.
Natels de Gottardi and David E. Righ
etti for many years conducted a general
merchandise store in Cayucos, a town fit
about 400 inhabitants. Despite humble
surroundings, the firm, did a Dig business
and turnea over many hundreds of dol
lars each week. On April 30, 1901. it was
indebted to merchants and Danks in tbe
sum of $30,000. De Gottardi came to, Lcs
Angeles on April 27 and collected about
$60w in cash from Loeb. f .eishman & Co.
This amount he claims to have put into
the firm's safe in t.'ayucos, together with
$1500 of his own money, the same night.
On the morning of May 1 De Gottar li
and Righetti announced that their safe
had been robbed by burglars, who ha>!
secured entrance to the store by drilling
the rear door. The safe was not damage 1
in the least.
Creditors Take a Hand.
San Francisco creditors sent for t.io
partners, and they were examined in that
city before a referee. The result was that
the creditors attached the store and
stock. Bankruptcy proceedings were in -
stituted, and the partners were ordered
to appear before Referee Louis Lamy m
San Luis Obispo.
In that examination ;t; t was ascertained
that De Gottardi had disposed of a one
half interest to his brother-in-law; that
he had drawn from the firm's cash $<55"0
within six weeks prior to the so-called
robbery; that Righetti had sold his in
terest in a dairy business for a $2000 note.
which he claims to have given to his
wife; that he also drew $1200 out of the
firm's business and purchased with it a
house and lot, which he immediately
homesteaded; that the firm on May 1,
after the alleged robbery, had paid to F.
A. Dorn. a San Luis Obispo attorney.
$2000, which the partners claimed was in
payment of an $800 bill, the $2000 being a
retainer for the burglary case.
During the first examination before the
referee both De Gottardi and Righetti re
fused to answer vital and important in
quiries on the ground that "my answer
might tend to incriminate me and might
be made the foundation of a criminal ac
tion against me, and it might have a ten
dency to subject me to punishment for a
crime."
Refuse to Answer Questions.
The referee grew tired of an empty pro
ceeding and brought the affair to the ears
of Judge Wellborn, who decided that the
questions must be answered.
When the examination was taken up
again in October De Gottardi was asked
what disposition he had made of the $6500
drawn from the firm's funds. The witness
replied that he had given tc a woman $6000
and to a physician $425.
"Who are these persons?" he was asked.
De Gottardi refused to answer. When
pressed regarding the mysterious woman
the father of five children informed the
court that he had got into some trouble
with her. That was the reason he had
given her the $6000. The witness would
not reveal more on this score and his re
fusal is a cause of the contempt case
now on.
Mrs. Righetti, wife of the partner, is up
for contempt also. She claims to have re
ceived a $2000 note from her husband, but
refused to reveal anything more regard
ing it.
No Evidence of Robbery.
Referee Lemy, after taking all the tes
timony, found:
Firstâ That there had been no robbery.
Secondâ That there had been no affair
with a woman and the subsequent pay
ment to her of $6000 by De Gottardi.
Thirdâ That there had been no $425 pay
ment to a physician.
On October 17 the referee, therefore, or
dered the firm to pay to the trustee the
sum of $7500; De Gottardi to pay the sum
of $5000, making in all a total of $12,500.
The two were given twenty-four hours in
which to put up the money in favor of
the creditors.
The orders were not obeyed by either
De Gottardi or Righetti. consequently
both are in the United States District
Court upon contempt proceedings, fight
ing for liberty.
Attorney F. A. Dorn was notified by the
referee to return the $2800 which the firm
had paid to him. He immediately com
plied with the reauest.
The contempt proceedings will occupy
several days, as much testimony is to be
reviewed.
Dental Examiners Meet.
A meeting of the State Board of Den
tal Examiners was held last Monday in
the office of Dr. F. G. Baird. 502 Sutter
street. Committees were appointed to
visit the various dental colleges In the
State and also prepare examinations for
students applying for admission.
The members of the board are: Presi
dent, H. R. Harbison, San Diego; secre
tary. Dr. F. G. Baird, San Francisco;
treasurer. Dr. F. J. Bethel; directorsâ
Dr. R, H. Cool, San Francisco; Dr. J. M.
Dunn, Oakland; Dr. G. E, Schellig,
Marysvllle. and Dr. T. R. Cunningham
of Los Angeles.
Buero Would Not Prosecute.
The case of John J. Devlin, dairyman
on the Mission road, charged with as
sault with a deadly weapon, was dis
missed by Judge Mogan yesterday. Dev
lin was accused of attempting to stab J.
Buero, a carpenter, in a saloon at the
Six-Mile House. After leaving the saloon,
it is alleged, he got a shotgun, returned
and stood outside, firing it off occasion
ally and daring Buero to come out. Buero
said yesterday that he and Devlin were
old friends and for his mother's sake he
would not prosecute him.

CORONER STOPS
THE INTERMENT
Orders Thorough Invest
igation of Hessman
Suicide.
Inquest Postponed Because
Wife Fails to Appear
as Witness.
ALAMEDA. Nov. 2<?.-Mrsi. ' Clara T.
Hessman,- widow of the late Otto Hess
man. who committed suicide early Sun
day morning, failed to put in an appear
ance thi3 evening at the local branch
Morgue at 5 o'clock, the time set for an
inquest into the cause of her husband.*
death.
In explanation of her absence. Coroner
H. B. Mehrmann received a note written
oil a. scrap of foolscap paper, dated to
day, bearing the name George Adam,
Mf D.. 1196 Green street, San Francisco,
and certifying that Mrs. Hes3rnan was
suffering Â¦ from nervous prostration and
would be unable to be present at the in
quest. â¢ . . . . â¢
After reading the note and conferring
with City Marshal John Conrad and Drs>.
C. L. Tlsdale and A. A. Stafford, Coroner
Mehrmann announced to the jurymen and
witnesses that he would not go on with
the inquest without Mrs. Hessman's pres
ence, or until he was thoroughly satisfied
that she could not attend. He then post
poned the inquiry until Thursday next at
10 a. m.. and ordered Drs. Tisdale an- 1
Stafford to perform an autopsy on Hes3
man's body, which had already bee.i
placed in a coffin and prepared for burial.
Coroner Mehrmann said':
I know of no good reason why Mrs. Hessman
could not be here this evening. She was regu
larly supenaed and was well Â«hlÂ» to be around
yesterday and early last evening. Later shÂ«
â¢ went over to San Francisco,, and I am informed
is now stopping at 736 Bush street. She wanted
the remains of her husband burled yesterday,
but 1 refused to allow this. Then she wanted
them interred to-day. I refused again and no- . .
tified Deputy Coroner James Fowler to hold the S
body until after the iiwuest.
Mrs. Mary Dickson. Mrs. Hessman's sister. .
came to me and desired to know if she could
not appear as a witness in her sister's stead
I told her "No." I intend to question Mrs
Hessmarf, if it is possible for me to do so. Lo
cal appearances indicate that Hessman died,
from gas asphyxiation, but I have a witness
who will testify that he saw Arthur Stenblht I ,
Jump upon Hessman's stomach with both feÂ«t J- .
after he . had Â¦ knocked him down In the fight
Saturday afternoon. It may be possible that: 1 ;*
injuries thus Inflicted had more to do with ; "?
causing Hessman's death than the gas had. I
mean to investigate this affair from beginning Q
to end, so that It cannot be said that anything
was left undone to determine how Hessman's
life came to an end.
Drs. Tisdale and Stafford in the autopsy
found that the internal organs in the ab
dominal and chest cavities were in fairly
good order. The face was marked with
numerous contusions caused by blows, but
no trace of a skull fracture or brain con
cussion was revealed. The lungs held con
siderable gas.
Arthur Stenbiht. who was. named as co
respondent in a cross-complaint filed by.
Hessman in the suit for divorce brought
by the latter' 3 w (Â°, was released from,
the City Prison late last night on bail put
up by his mother. Stenbiht was arrested
on complaint of Detective George Brown
for the part he took In the brawl with
Hessman last Saturday and charged with
disturbing the peace. He will be given a
hearing to-morrow morning at 10 o'clock
by Justice Morris. Stenbiht called at the
branch Morgue this morning to ascertain
whether he would be wanted as a wit
ness at the inquest. He will be subpo
naeds

lUJSSIAN FIRM AFTER
AN ABSCONDING CLERK
Ccurts Asked to Prevent Valentine
Semashka From Drawing 1 Money
From Bank.
The firm of E. L,. Zeefo & Co. of Rostov
on the Don, Russia, through its represen
tative here, applied to the Superior Court
yesterday for an order restraining the
Anglo-Californian Bank from paying to
Valentine Semashka any portion of the
sum of $297 79 which Semashka has on de
posit at the bank and from recognizing
any claim Attorney George D. Collins may
have upon the sum.
The complaint filed Is a lengthy one
and in it is alleged that Semashka Is an
embezzler, that his real name is Simeon
Ivanov Grin and that the sum on deposit
in the bank is part of 25,000 rubles
(sl2 500) which he stole from the firm of E.
L-. Zeefo & Co. while he was in its em
ploy In Russia. It is alleged that he was
given the money to deposit with the ad
ministrator of the Vladkavkaz Railroad in
March of this year and that instead of
doing so he absconded, taking the funds
with him. They also alleges that in order
to save the deposit he has made an assign
ment to Attorney George D. Collins.

Co-Eds Plan for Woman's Day.
BERKELEY, Nov. 26.â The women stu
dents of the University of California are
planning for a series of events to occur
on woman's day, February 22. The com
mittee in charge, through its chairman,
Miss Lucille Hewitt, has suggested that
the finals of the tennis tournament and a
basket-ball game be held in the morning
in the basket-ball court. These events
are to be for a feminine audience exclu
sively, but in the afternoon an extrava
ganza is planned to be given in the court
which men and women may attend. In
the evening It is proposed to hold a colo
nial party or a large reception in Hearst
Hall.
The women students have taken the
committee's suggestions under considera
tion and will decide later whether or not
to accept them.

OIL RATE CASES
BEING ARGUED
Attorney Sterry Makes
Opening Statement
for Railroad.
:--:'Â¦ :Â¦. ' â¢Â¦/â¢>Â¦Â¦â¢Â¦Â¦Â¦- .^- ; - v.t
He Claims That the Commis
sion Has Exceeded Its
- < â¢' â¢'â¢ Authority. Â¦
All the evidence in the oil rate cases
taken before Railroad Commissioners
Laumeister, Edson and Blackstock ha 3
been made a part of the record and the
arguments have begun. W. A. Worthing
ton testified yesterday afternoon, and
when his cross-examination was conclui
ed by Frank N. Short. Attorney Sterry
began his argument In behalf of the San
ta Fe road.
Worthlngton is the chief statistician of
the Southern Pacific operating depart
ment, and was armed with figures. Ac
cording to his testimony the company's
heaviest locomotive weighs twenty-six
tons and is capable of hauling 850 tons.
The smallest of the locomotives has a
weight of fifteen tons. Trains on the Val
ley division run from 400 to 800 tons, the
average of which was placed at 658 tons
on the line from Bakersfield to San Fran
Cisco. Out of this 658 tons 313 tons are
actual paying freight. Worthington's fig
ures showed a cost of $1 84 per ton
mile, minus interest, and $1 10 for return
ing the empties. Worthington concluded
with a lengthy statement on the grades
between Bakersfield a.id Oakland.
Sterry's argument v.-Â«is on the lines of
railroad justice and fairness in fixing the
rates. He claimed that the railroad com
panies, had a right to fix such rates as
would give them a raasonable profit on
the money invested, r.nTf that the Commis
sioners did not have the right to say that
8 cents a barrel on oil was too much u-\
less they compared '.t with every other
commodity. Sterry citod legal opinions
to support his contention. When he
reached the "fair-minded Commissioners"
he raised his voice on high against Tne
Call editorial of Tuesday morning.
~ The case went over until Saturday
morning.
GOES TO JAIL FOB.
HIS SISTES'S HONOR
Fred Scott Declares He Accepts Pun
ishment Because He Defended
His Family Name.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â Fred Scott, the
young colored man who was convicted a
â¢week ago for assault with a deadly wea
pon with intent to kill Harry "Wilson, also
colored, was sentenced by Judge Melvin
to-day to serve two years in the County
Jail for the offense. Just before passing
sentence Judge Melvin, as is customary,
asked the prisoner if he had anything to
say for himself.
"Yes," said the prisoner, rising and
standing in the middle of the courtroom.
"I wish to say that I stand convicted of
a crime committed while I was trying to
protect the name and honor of my sister
and family, and under the circumstances
I will gladly go to prison."
Scott expressed a preference for tbe
County Jail rather than one of the State
prisons, and Judge Melvin granted his re
quest to be sent there. Since his incar
ceration in the County Jail Scott has
been constantly visited by his sister and
parents.
JUSTICE OF THE PEACE
PEBFOBMS THE CEBEMONY
Miss Edna G. Karman and Louis Raf
fetto Jr. Are Quietly Married
by Judge Quinn.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â A quiet wedding
took place this morning in the chambers
of Justice of the Peace Quinn, the prin
cipals being Miss Edna G. Karman and
Louis Raffetto Jr. The bride has been
a resident of Oakland but a short time,
coming to this city with her parents
about five months ago from Grand Rap
ids, Mich., but the time was long enough
for Mr. Raffetto â¢to woo and win her.
The groom is we'll known here, having
lived in Oakland nearly all his life. He
is a son of Louis" Raffetto St., a retired
capitalist.
After the ceremony a wedding break
fast was served at the residence of the
bride's parents, 950 Fifth street. A recep
tion followed and later the young people
took their departure for San Jose, where
they will spend the honeymoon. On their
return they will reside at 950 Fifth street,
where Mr. Raffetto has fitted up a home
for his bride.
CITY TAXES YET UNPAID
ARE NOW DELINQUENT
Large Sum Will Remain Outstanding
Until Courts Settle Assessment
Difficulties.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â The first Install
ment of city taxes unpaid is now delin
quent and from to-day on a penalty
charge will be exacted!* The amount due
the city under the first installemnt Is
$301,015 82. About $200,000 has been collect
ed, leaving a delinquency of about $100,000.
This is caused largely by refusals to
pay on the increased assessments on busi
ness property. The revenue from this in
crease would be about $15,000, but more
than four times that amount is tied up
pending the settlement of the legal points
upon which the refusals are based.
RUNAWAY TEAM CAUSES
DISASTER IN HAYWARDS
Mrs. Carl Nissen and Miss Emma
Pestdorf Sustain Injuries in a
Road Collision.
OAKLAND, Nov. 26.â Mrs. Carl Nissen
and her sister, Miss Emma Pestdorf,
were to-day thrown fr^m their buggy In
Haywards and seriously injured. The la
dies were driving along B street when
they were overtaken by a runaway team
attached to a wagon. The pole of the
wagon smashed in the back of the buggy,
and the force of the collision broke off a
hind wheel of the ladies' rig and thrtw
the occupants into the street.
